$fader = $(this);\n\t    setTimeout(function() {\n\t      $fader.addClass('fade_this_in');\n\t    }, i*25+150);\n\t\t});\n</script></p>\n<h2 id='the-march-of-innovation-continues'>The March of Innovation Continues</h2>\n<p>Just a few weeks ago it was <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2021/10/celebrating-a-third-of-a-century-of-mathematica-and-looking-forward/'>1/3 of a century since Mathematica 1.0 was released</a>. Today Im excited to announce the latest results of our long-running R&#038;D pipeline: Version 13 of <a href='https://www.wolfram.com/language/'>Wolfram Language</a> and <a href='https://www.wolfram.com/mathematica/'>Mathematica</a>. (Yes, the <a href='https://blog.wolfram.com/2021/11/09/13-ways-of-looking-at-the-number-13/'>1, 3 theme</a>complete with the fact that its the 13th of the month todayis amusing, if coincidental.)</p>\n<p>Its 207 daysor a little over 6 monthssince we released <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2021/05/launching-version-12-3-of-wolfram-language-mathematica/'>Version 12.3</a>. And Im pleased to say that in that short time an impressive amount of R&#038;D has come to fruition: not only a total of <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/SummaryOfNewFeaturesIn130.html'>117 completely new functions</a>, but also many hundreds of updated and upgraded functions, several thousand bug fixes and small enhancements, and a host of new ideas to make the system ever easier and smoother to use.</p>\n<p>Every day, every week, every month for the past third of a century weve been pushing hard to add more to the vast integrated framework that is Mathematica and the Wolfram Language. And now we can see the results of all those individual ideas and projects and pieces of work: a steady drumbeat of innovation sustained now over the course of more than a third of a century:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/1sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='623' height='319' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/1sw1213img1_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='1_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p><span id=\"more-33886\"></span></p>\n<p>This plot reflects lots of hard work. But it also reflects something else: the success of the <a href='https://www.wolfram.com/language/principles/'>core design principles</a> of the Wolfram Language. Because those are what have allowed what is now a huge system to maintain its coherence and consistencyand to grow ever stronger. What we build today is not built from scratch; it is built on top of the <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/'>huge tower of capabilities</a> that we have built before. And that is why were able to reach so far, automate so muchand invent so much. </p>\n<p>In <a href='https://www.wolfram.com/mathematica/scrapbook/'>Version 1.0</a> there were a total of <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/legacy/v1/'>554 functions</a> altogether. Yet between Version 12.0 and Version 13.0 weve now added a <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/SummaryOfNewFeaturesIn130.html'>total of 635 new functions</a> (in addition to the 702 functions that have been updated and upgraded). And its actually even more impressive than that. Because when we add a function today the expectations are so much higher than in 1988because theres so much more automation we can do, and so much more in the whole system that we have to connect to and integrate with. And, of course, today we can and do write perhaps a hundred times more extensive and detailed <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/'>documentation</a> than would have ever fit in the (printed) <em><a href='https://www.stephenwolfram.com/publications/mathematica-book/'>Mathematica Book</a></em> of 1988.</p>\n<p>The complete span of whats <a href='https://www.wolfram.com/mathematica/new-in-13/'>new in Version 13</a> relative to <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2019/04/version-12-launches-today-big-jump-for-wolfram-language-and-mathematica/'>Version 12</a> is very large and impressive. But here Ill just concentrate on whats new in Version 13.0 relative to Version 12.3; Ive written before about <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2020/03/in-less-than-a-year-so-much-new-launching-version-12-1-of-wolfram-language-mathematica/'>Version 12.1</a>, <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2020/12/launching-version-12-2-of-wolfram-language-mathematica-228-new-functions-and-much-more/'>Version 12.2</a> and <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2021/05/launching-version-12-3-of-wolfram-language-mathematica/'>Version 12.3</a>.</p>\n<h2 id='dont-forget-integrals'>Don&#8217;t Forget Integrals!</h2>\n<p>Back in 1988 one of the features of <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/legacy/v1/'>Mathematica 1.0</a> that people really liked was the ability to do integrals symbolically. Over the years, weve gradually increased the range of integrals that can be done. And a third of a century laterin Version 13.0were delivering another jump forward. </p>\n<p>Heres an integral that couldnt be done in <a href='https://www.wolframscience.com/nks/notes-12-6--history-of-exact-solutions/'>closed form</a> before, but in Version 13.0 it can: </p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/02sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='620' height='287' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/02sw1213img1_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='02-1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='02-1_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Any integral of an algebraic function can in principle be done in terms of our general <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/DifferentialRoot.html'>DifferentialRoot</a></tt> objects. But the bigger algorithmic challenge is to get a human-friendly answer in terms of familiar functions. Its a fragile business, where a small change in a coefficient can have a large effect on what reductions are possible. But in Version 13.0 there are now many integrals that could previously be done only in terms of special functions, but now give results in elementary functions. Heres an example:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/02sw1213img2.png\" alt='' title='' width='392' height='130' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/02sw1213img2_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='02-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='02-2_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>In Version 12.3 the same integral could still be done, but only in terms of <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/EllipticIntegrals.html'>elliptic integrals</a>:</p>\n<p><img class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/02sw1213img03.png\" alt='Elliptic integrals' title='Elliptic integrals' width='620' height='' /></p>\n<h2 id='mathematical-functions-a-milestone-is-reached'>Mathematical Functions: A Milestone Is Reached</h2>\n<p>Back when one still had to do integrals and the like by hand, it was always a thrill when one discovered that ones problem could be solved in terms of some exotic <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/SpecialFunctions.html'>special function</a> that one hadnt even heard of before. Special functions are in a sense a way of <a href='https://www.stephenwolfram.com/publications/history-future-special-functions/'>packaging mathematical knowledge</a>: once you know that the solution to your equation is a <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/HeunAndRelatedFunctions.html#471725295'>Lam function</a>, that immediately tells you lots of mathematical things about it.</p>\n<p>In the <a href='https://www.wolfram.com/language/'>Wolfram Language</a>, weve always taken special functions very seriously, not only supporting a vast collection of them, but also making it possible to evaluate them to <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/howto/ControlThePrecisionAndAccuracyOfNumericalResults.html'>any numerical precision</a>, and to have them participate in a full range of symbolic mathematical operations. </p>\n<p>When I first started using special functions about 45 years ago, the book that was the standard reference was <em><a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abramowitz_and_Stegun' target=_blank rel=noopener>Abramowitz &#038; Steguns 1964 Handbook of Mathematical Functions</a></em>. It listed hundreds of functions, some widely used, others less so. And over the years in the development of Wolfram Language weve steadily been checking off <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/MathematicalFunctions.html'>more functions</a> from <em>Abramowitz &#038; Stegun</em>. </p>\n<p>And in Version 13.0 were finally done! All the functions in <em>Abramowitz &#038; Stegun</em> are now fully computable in the Wolfram Language. The last functions to be added were the <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/SpecialFunctions.html#10588913'>Coulomb wavefunctions</a> (relevant for studying quantum scattering processes). Here they are in <em>Abramowitz &#038; Stegun</em>:</p>\n<p><img class='aligncenter' src='https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/03sw1213-abramiwitz-stegun-book.png' alt='Abramowitz &#038; Stegun' title='Abramowitz &#038; Stegun' width='620' height=' '></p>\n<p>And heresas of Version 13how to get that first picture in Wolfram Language: </p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/03sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='580' height='230' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='03-1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='03-1_out_text'>Plot[{CoulombF[l,1,10],CoulombG[l,1,10]},{l,0,15},PlotRange->{-1.2,1.8}]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Of course theres more to the story, as we can now see:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/03sw1213img2.png\" alt='' title='' width='413' height='233' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='03-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='03-2_out_text'>Plot[{CoulombF[l,1,10],CoulombG[l,1,10]},{l,0,15}]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<h2 id='another-kind-of-number'>Another Kind of Number</h2>\n<p>One might think that a number is just a number. And thats basically true for integers. But when a number is a real number the story is more complicated. Sometimes you can name a real number symbolically, say <span class='InlineFormula'><img src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/04sw1213img1.png\" width= '16' height='19' align='absmiddle'></span>. But most real numbers dont have symbolic names. And to specify them exactly youd have to give an infinite number of digits, or the equivalent. And the result is that one ends up wanting to have approximate real numbers that one can think of as representing certain whole collections of actual real numbers.</p>\n<p>A straightforward way of doing this is to use finite-precision numbers, as in:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/04sw1213img2.png\" alt='' title='' width='435' height='54' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/04sw1213img2_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='04-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='04-2_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Another approachintroduced in Version 12.0is <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/Around.html'>Around</a></tt>, which in effect represents a distribution of numbers randomly distributed around a given number:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/04sw1213img3.png\" alt='' title='' width='166' height='54' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/04sw1213img3_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='04-3_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='04-3_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>When you do operations on <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/Around.html'>Around</a></tt> numbers the errors are combined using a <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2019/04/version-12-launches-today-big-jump-for-wolfram-language-and-mathematica/#calculus-of-uncertainty'>certain calculus of errors</a> thats effectively based on Gaussian distributionsand the results you get are always in some sense statistical.</p>\n<p>But what if you want to use approximate numbers, but still get provable results? One approach is to use <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/Interval.html'>Interval</a></tt>. But a more streamlined approach now available in Version 13.0 is to use <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/CenteredInterval.html'>CenteredInterval</a></tt>. Heres a <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/CenteredInterval.html'>CenteredInterval</a></tt> used as input to a <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/BesselRelatedFunctions.html'>Bessel function</a>: </p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/04sw1213img4.png\" alt='' title='' width='308' height='64' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/04sw1213img4_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='04-4_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='04-4_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>You can prove things in the Wolfram Language in many ways. You can use <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/Reduce.html'>Reduce</a></tt>. You can use <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/FindEquationalProof.html'>FindEquationalProof</a></tt>. And you can use <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/CenteredInterval.html'>CenteredInterval</a></tt>which in effect leverages numerical evaluation. Heres a function that has complicated transcendental roots:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/04sw1213img5.png\" alt='' title='' width='278' height='184' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='04-5_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='04-5_out_text'>Plot[Gamma[x]-Sin[x],{x,1,4}]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Can we prove that the function is above 0 between 3 and 4? Lets evaluate the function over a centered interval there:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/04sw1213img6.png\" alt='' title='' width='431' height='57' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='04-6_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='04-6_out_text'>With[{x=CenteredInterval[7/2,1/2]},Gamma[x]-Sin[x]]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Now we can check that indeed all of this interval is greater than 0:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/04sw1213img7.png\" alt='' title='' width='79' height='51' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='04-7_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='04-7_out_text'>%>0</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>And from the worst-case way the interval was computed this now provides a definite theorem.</p>\n<h2 id='as-well-as-lots-of-other-math'>As Well As Lots of Other Math&#8230;</h2>\n<p>As in every new version of the <a href='https://www.wolfram.com/language/'>Wolfram Language</a>, Version 13.0 has lots of specific mathematical enhancements. An example is a new, convenient way to get the <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/FunctionPoles.html'>poles of a function</a>. Heres a particular function plotted in the complex plane:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='301' height='218' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img1_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='05-1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='05-1_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>And here are the exact poles (and their multiplicities) for this function within the unit circle:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img2.png\" alt='' title='' width='608' height='114' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img2_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='05-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='05-2_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Now we can sum the residues at these poles and use <a href='https://mathworld.wolfram.com/CauchyIntegralTheorem.html'>Cauchys theorem</a> to get a contour integral:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img3.png\" alt='' title='' width='533' height='80' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img3_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='05-3_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='05-3_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Also in the area of calculus weve added various conveniences to the handling of differential equations. For example, we now directly support <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/DSolveValue.html'>vector variables in ODEs</a>:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img4.png\" alt='' title='' width='550' height='90' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img4_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='05-4_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='05-4_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Using our graph theory capabilities weve also been able to greatly enhance our handling of systems of ODEs, finding ways to untangle them into block-diagonal forms that allow us to find symbolic solutions in much more complex cases than before.</p>\n<p>For PDEs its typically not possible to get general closed-form solutions for nonlinear PDEs. But sometimes one can get particular solutions known as <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/CompleteIntegral.html'>complete integrals</a> (in which there are just arbitrary constants, not whole arbitrary functions). And now we have an explicit function for finding these:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img5.png\" alt='' title='' width='621' height='61' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img5_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='05-5_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='05-5_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Turning from calculus to algebra, weve added the function <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/PolynomialSumOfSquaresList.html'>PolynomialSumOfSquaresList</a></tt> that provides a kind of certificate of positivity for a multivariate polynomial. The idea is that if a polynomial can be decomposed into a sum of squares (and most, but not all, that are never negative can) then this proves that the polynomial is indeed always non-negative: </p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img6.png\" alt='' title='' width='486' height='89' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img6_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='05-6_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='05-6_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>And, yes, summing the squares gives the original polynomial again:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/05sw1213img7.png\" alt='' title='' width='212' height='51' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='05-7_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='05-7_out_text'>%.%//Expand</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>In Version 13.0 weve also added a couple of new matrix functions. Theres <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/Adjugate.html'>Adjugate</a></tt>, which is essentially a matrix inverse, but without dividing by the determinant. And theres <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/DrazinInverse.html'>DrazinInverse</a></tt> which gives the inverse of the nonsingular part of a matrixas used particularly in solving differential-algebraic equations. </p>\n<h2 id='more-pde-modeling-solid-structural-mechanics'>More PDE Modeling: Solid &#038; Structural Mechanics</h2>\n<p>PDEs are both difficult to solve and difficult to set up for particular situations. Over the course of many years weve built state-of-the-art finite-element solution capabilities for PDEs. Weve also built our groundbreaking <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/GeometricComputation.html'>symbolic computational geometry system</a> that lets us flexibly describe regions for PDEs. But starting in <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2020/12/launching-version-12-2-of-wolfram-language-mathematica-228-new-functions-and-much-more/'>Version 12.2</a> weve done something else too: weve started creating explicit symbolic modeling frameworks for particular kinds of physical systems that can be modeled with PDEs. Weve already got <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/PDEModels/tutorial/HeatTransfer/HeatTransfer.html'>heat transfer</a>, <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/PDEModels/tutorial/MassTransport/MassTransport.html'>mass transport</a> and <a href='https://reference.wolframcloud.com/language/PDEModels/tutorial/PDEModelsOverview.html' target=_blank rel=noopener>acoustics</a>. Now in Version 13.0 were adding solid and structural mechanics.</p>\n<p>For us a <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2020/12/launching-version-12-2-of-wolfram-language-mathematica-228-new-functions-and-much-more/#convenient-real-world-pdes'>classic test problem has been the deflection of a teaspoon</a>. Heres how we can now set that up. First we need to define our variables: the displacements of the spoon in each direction at each <em>x</em>, <em>y</em>, <em>z</em> point:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='359' height='21' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='06-1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='06-1_out_text'>vars={{u[x,y,z],v[x,y,z],w[x,y,z]},{x,y,z}};</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Then we need to say what the material parameters of our spoon are. And here we get to make use of our whole knowledgebase, which contains detailed information on many kinds of materials:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img2.png\" alt='' title='' width='366' height='27' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img2_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='06-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='06-2_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Now were ready to actually set up and solve the PDE problem:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img3.png\" alt='' title='' width='559' height='384' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img3_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='06-3_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='06-3_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>The result is given as a list of <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/InterpolatingFunction.html'>interpolating functions</a> for the <em>x</em>, <em>y</em>, <em>z</em> displacements. Now we can use a new Version 13.0 graphics function to immediately visualize this result:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img4.png\" alt='' title='' width='493' height='303' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img4_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='06-4_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='06-4_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>But conveniently packaged in those interpolation functions is also lots more detail about the solution we got. For example, heres the strain tensor for the spoon, given as a symmetrized array of interpolating functions:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img5.png\" alt='' title='' width='426' height='106' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='06-5_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='06-5_out_text'>strains=SolidMechanicsStrain[vars,pars,displacement]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>And now we can for example find the maximum 3, 3 component of the strain tensor and the position where its achieved:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img6.png\" alt='' title='' width='476' height='76' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img6_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='06-6_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='06-6_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>How about finding the distribution of values of the strain over the spoon? One easy way to do that is just to sample random points in the spoon</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img7.png\" alt='' title='' width='581' height='102' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img7_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='06-7_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='06-7_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>and then to make a smoothed histogram of the strains at those points:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img8.png\" alt='' title='' width='500' height='329' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/06sw1213img8_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='06-8_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='06-8_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>(The maximum we saw before is in the tail on the right.)</p>\n<p>Solid mechanics is a complicated area, and what we have in Version 13 is good, industrial-grade technology for handling it. And in fact we have a whole monograph titled <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/PDEModels/tutorial/StructuralMechanics/SolidMechanicsVerificationTests.html'>&#8220;Solid Mechanics Model Verification&#8221;</a> that describes how weve validated our results. We are also providing a general monograph on solid mechanics that describes how to take particular problems and solve them with our technology stack.</p>\n<h2 id='making-videos-from-images-videos'>Making Videos from Images &#038; Videos</h2>\n<p>In <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2021/05/launching-version-12-3-of-wolfram-language-mathematica/'>Version 12.3</a> we released functions like <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/AnimationVideo.html'>AnimationVideo</a></tt> and <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/SlideShowVideo.html'>SlideShowVideo</a></tt> which make it easy to produce videos from generated content. In Version 13.0 we now also have a collection of functions for creating videos from existing images, and videos.</p>\n<p>By the way, before we even get to making videos, another important new feature in Version 13.0 is that its now possible to play videos directly in a notebook:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/07sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='303' height='27' /><br />\n\t\t\t\t\t<video loading='lazy' height=\"auto\" width=\"360\" style=\"margin-left:0px\" muted=\"\" alt=\"\" title=\"\" src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/07sw1213-video01.mp4\" autoplay=\"\" loop=\"\" playsinline><br />\n</video>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='07-1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='07-1_out_text'>Video[\"ExampleData/Caminandes.mp4\"]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>This works both on the desktop and in the cloud, and you get all the standard video controls right in the notebook, but you can also pop out the video to view it with an external (say, full-screen) viewer. (You can also now just wrap a video with <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/AnimatedImage.html'>AnimatedImage</a></tt> to make it into a GIF-like frame-based animation.)</p>\n<p>OK, so back to making videos from images. Lets say you have a large image:</p>\n<p><img loading='lazy' class='' src='https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/07sw1213-tour-video-image.png' alt='Tour video image' title='Tour video image' width='358' height=' '/></p>\n<p>A good way to experience an image like this can be through a tour video that visits different parts of the image in turn. Heres an example of how to do that:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/07sw1213img2.png\" alt='' title='' width='620' height='255' /><br />\n\t\t\t\t\t<img src='https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/07sw1213-video2.gif' alt=' ' width='292' height=' '>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='07-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='07-2_out_text'>TourVideo[CloudGet[\"https://wolfr.am/10Dku2BIV\"],<|\"Position\"->{Scaled[{.1,.5}],Scaled[{.9,.5}]},\"Width\"->2000|>,RasterSize->250]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>You 2]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>We can also take a single video and summarize it as a series of video + audio snippets, chosen for example equally spaced in the video. Think of it as a video version of <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/VideoFrameList.html'>VideoFrameList</a></tt>. Heres an example summarizing a 75-minute video: </p>\n<p><img src='https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/07sw1213-video006.gif' alt=' ' width='426' height=' '></p>\n<p>There are some practical conveniences for handling videos that have been added in Version 13.0. One is <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/OverlayVideo.html'>OverlayVideo</a></tt> which allows you to watermark a video with an image, or insert what amounts to a picture-in-picture video:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img src='https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/07sw1213-video7.gif' alt=' ' width='542' height=' '>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/07sw1213img009_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='07-9_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='07-9_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Weve also made many image operations directly work on videos. So, for example, to crop a video, you just need to use <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/ImageCrop.html'>ImageCrop</a></tt>:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/07sw1213img11.png\" alt='' title='' width='272' height='199' /><br />\n\t\t\t\t\t<img src='https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/07sw1213-video8.gif' alt=' ' width='284' height=' '>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='07-11_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='07-11_out_text'>ImageCrop[%,200]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<h2 id='image-stitching'>Image Stitching</h2>\n<p>Lets say youve taken a bunch of pictures at different anglesand now you want to stitch them together. In Version 13.0 weve made that very easywith the function <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/ImageStitch.html'>ImageStitch</a></tt>:</p>\n<p><img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/08sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='642' height='' /></p>\n<p>Part of whats under the hood in image stitching is finding key points in images. And in Version 13.0 weve added two further methods (SIFT and RootSIFT) for <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/ImageKeypoints.html'>ImageKeypoints</a></tt>. But aligning key points isnt the only thing were doing in image stitching. Were also doing things like brightness equalization and lens correction, as well as blending images across seams. </p>\n<p>Image stitching can be refined using options like <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/TransformationClass.html'>TransformationClass</a></tt>which specify what transformations should be allowed when the separate images are assembled.</p>\n<h2 id='trees-continue-to-grow'>Trees Continue to Grow</h2>\n<p>We introduced <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/Tree.html'>Tree</a></tt> as a basic construct in <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2021/05/launching-version-12-3-of-wolfram-language-mathematica/'>Version 12.3</a>. In 13.0 were extending <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/Tree.html'>Tree</a></tt> and adding some enhancements. First of all, there are now options for tree layout and visualization. </p>\n<p>For example, this lays out a tree radially (note that knowing its a tree rather than a general graph makes it possible to do much more systematic embeddings):</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/09sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='509' height='336' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='09-1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='09-1_out_text'>GraphTree[CompleteKaryTree[5],TreeLayout->\"RadialEmbedding\"]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>This adds options for styling elements, with one particular element specified by its tree position being singled out as blue:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/09sw1213img2.png\" alt='' title='' width='610' height='334' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='09-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='09-2_out_text'>GraphTree[CompleteKaryTree[5],TreeLayout->\"RadialEmbedding\",TreeElementLabel->(All->None),TreeElementStyle->{{1,1,1}->Blue,All->Red}]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>One of the more sophisticated new tree concepts is <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/TreeTraversalOrder.html'>TreeTraversalOrder</a></tt>. Imagine youre going to map across a tree. In what order should you visit the nodes? Heres the default behavior. Set up a tree:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/09sw1213img3.png\" alt='' title='' width='286' height='148' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='09-3_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='09-3_out_text'>GraphTree[KaryTree[10,3]]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Now show in which order the nodes are visited by <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/TreeScan.html'>TreeScan</a></tt>:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/09sw1213img4.png\" alt='' title='' width='179' height='271' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='09-4_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='09-4_out_text'>TreeScan[Echo,%]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>This explicitly labels the nodes in the order they are visited:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/09sw1213img5.png\" alt='' title='' width='418' height='251' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/09sw1213img5_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='09-5_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='09-5_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>This order is by default depth first. But now <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/TreeTraversalOrder.html'>TreeTraversalOrder</a></tt> allows you to ask for other orderings. Heres breadth-first order:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/09sw1213img6.png\" alt='' title='' width='616' height='227' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/09sw1213img6_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='09-6_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='09-6_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Heres a slightly more ornate ordering:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/09sw1213img07.png\" alt='' title='' width='703' height='254' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/09sw1213img7_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='09-7_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='09-7_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Why does this matter? Traversal order turns out to be related to deep questions about evaluation processes and what I now call <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2021/09/even-beyond-physics-introducing-multicomputation-as-a-fourth-general-paradigm-for-theoretical-science/'>multicomputation</a>. In a sense a traversal order defines the reference frame through which an observer of the tree samples it. And, yes, that language sounds like physics, and for a good reason: this is all deeply related to a bunch of concepts about fundamental physics that arise in our <a href='https://www.wolframphysics.org/' target=_blank rel=noopener>Physics Project</a>. And the parametrization of traversal orderapart from being useful for a bunch of existing algorithmsbegins to open the door to connecting computational processes to ideas from physics, and new notions about what Im calling <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2021/09/even-beyond-physics-introducing-multicomputation-as-a-fourth-general-paradigm-for-theoretical-science/'>multicomputation</a>.</p>\n<h2 id='graph-coloring'>Graph Coloring</h2>\n<p>The graph theory capabilities of <a href='https://www.wolfram.com/language/'>Wolfram Language</a> have been very impressive for a long time (and were critical, for example, in making possible our <a href='https://www.wolframphysics.org/' target=_blank rel=noopener>Physics Project</a>). But in Version 13.0 were adding still more.</p>\n<p>A commonly requested set of capabilities revolve around <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/GraphPropertiesAndMeasurements.html#1047298221'>graph coloring</a>. For example, given a graph, how can one assign colors to its vertices so that no pair of adjacent vertices have the same color? In Version 13.0 theres a function <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/FindVertexColoring.html'>FindVertexColoring</a></tt> that does that:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='276' height='122' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img1_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='10-1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='10-1_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>And now we can highlight the graph with those colors: </p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img2.png\" alt='' title='' width='503' height='151' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img2_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='10-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='10-2_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>The classic graph coloring problem involves coloring geographic maps. So here, for example, is the graph representing the bordering relations for US states:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img3.png\" alt='' title='' width='470' height='226' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img3_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='10-3_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='10-3_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Now its an easy matter to find a 4-coloring of US states:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img4.png\" alt='' title='' width='536' height='341' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img4_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='10-4_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='10-4_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>There are actually a remarkable range of problems that can be reduced to graph coloring. Another example has to do with scheduling a tournament in which all pairs of people play each other, but everyone plays only one match at a time. The collection of matches needed is just the complete graph:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img5.png\" alt='' title='' width='179' height='165' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='10-5_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='10-5_out_text'>CompleteGraph[8]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Each match corresponds to an edge in the graph:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img6.png\" alt='' title='' width='637' height='81' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='10-6_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='10-6_out_text'>EdgeList[CompleteGraph[8]]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>And now by finding an edge coloring we have a list of possible time slots in which each match can be played:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img7.png\" alt='' title='' width='483' height='51' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/EdgeChromaticNumber.html'>EdgeChromaticNumber</a></tt> tells one the total number of matches needed:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td id='10-8_out_text'>EdgeChromaticNumber[CompleteGraph[8]]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Map coloring brings up the subject of planar graphs. Version 13.0 introduces new functions for working with planar graphs. <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/PlanarFaceList.html'>PlanarFaceList</a></tt> takes a planar graph and tells us how the graph can be decomposed into faces:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img9.png\" alt='' title='' width='594' height='154' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img9_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='10-9_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='10-9_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p><tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/FindPlanarColoring.html'>FindPlanarColoring</a></tt> directly computes a coloring for these planar faces. Meanwhile, <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/DualPlanarGraph.html'>DualPlanarGraph</a></tt> makes a graph in which each face is a vertex:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img10.png\" alt='' title='' width='263' height='306' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/10sw1213img10_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='10-10_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='10-10_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<h2 id='subgraph-isomorphism-more'>Subgraph Isomorphism &#038; More</h2>\n<p>It comes up all over the place. (In fact, in our <a href='https://www.wolframphysics.org/' target=_blank rel=noopener>Physics Project</a> its even something the universe is effectively doing throughout the network that represents space.) Where does a given <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/FindIsomorphicSubgraph.html'>graph contain a certain subgraph</a>? In Version 13.0 theres a function to determine that (the <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/All.html'>All</a></tt> says to give all instances): </p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/11sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='514' height='155' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/11sw1213img1_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='11-1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='11-1_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>A typical area where this kind of subgraph isomorphism comes up is in chemistry. Here is the graph structure for a molecule:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/11sw1213img2.png\" alt='' title='' width='450' height='302' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/11sw1213img2_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='11-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='11-2_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Now we can find a 6-cycle:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/11sw1213img03.png\" alt='' title='' width='341' height='135' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='11-3_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='11-3_out_text'>FindIsomorphicSubgraph[%,CycleGraph[6]]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Another new capability in Version 13.0 has to do with <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/GraphModifications.html#2145780989'>handling flow graphs</a>. The basic question is: in flowing through the graph, what vertices are critical, in the sense that they have to be visited if ones going to get to all future vertices? Heres an example of a directed graph (yes, made from a <a href='https://www.wolframphysics.org/technical-introduction/the-updating-process-in-our-models/multiway-systems-for-our-models/' target=_blank rel=noopener>multiway system</a>):</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' id='11-4_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='11-4_out_text'>ResourceFunction[\"MultiwaySystem\"][{\"A\"->\"BBB\",\"BB\"->\"A\"},\"A\",6,\"StatesGraph\"]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Now we can ask for the <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/DominatorTreeGraph.html'><tt>DominatorTreeGraph</tt></a>, which shows us a map of what vertices are critical to reach where, starting from A: </p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' dominator is, i.e. what the nearest critical vertex to it is:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/11sw1213img6.png\" alt='' title='' width='607' height='59' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='11-6_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='11- 6_out_text'>DominatorVertexList[%%,\"A\"]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>If the graph represents causal or other dependency of one event on others, the dominators are effectively synchronization points, where everything has to proceed through one thread of history.</p>\n<h2 id='estimations-of-spatial-fields'>Estimations of Spatial Fields</h2>\n<p>Imagine youve got data sampled at certain points in space, say on the surface of the Earth. The data might be from weather stations, soil samples, mineral drilling, or many other things. In Version 13.0 weve added a collection of functions for <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/SpatialStatistics.html#914095272'>estimating spatial fields</a> from samples (or whats sometimes known as <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kriging' target=_blank rel=noopener>kriging</a>).</p>\n<p>Lets take some sample data, and plot it:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/12sw1213img1.png\" id='12-1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='12-1_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Now lets make a spatial estimate of the data:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/12sw1213img2.png\" id='12-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='12-2_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>This behaves much like an <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/InterpolatingFunction.html'>InterpolatingFunction</a></tt>, which we can sample anywhere we want:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/12sw1213img3.png\" alt='' title='' width='315' height='248' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='12-3_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='12-3_out_text'>Plot3D[est[{x,y}],{x,0,3},{y,0,2}]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>To create this estimate, weve inevitably used a model. We can change the model when we create the spatial estimate:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/12sw1213img4.png\" alt='' title='' width='601' height='125' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/12sw1213img4_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='12-4_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='12-4_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Now the results will be different:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/12sw1213img5.png\" alt='' title='' width='275' height='212' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='12-5_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='12-5_out_text'>Plot3D[%[{x,y}],{x,0,3},{y,0,2}]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>In Version 13.0 you can get detailed control of the model by using options like <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/SpatialTrendFunction.html'>SpatialTrendFunction</a></tt> and <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/SpatialNoiseLevel.html'>SpatialNoiseLevel</a></tt>. A key issue is what to assume about local variations in the spatial fieldwhich you can specify in symbolic form using <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/VariogramModel.html'>VariogramModel</a></tt>.</p>\n<h2 id='getting-time-right-leap-seconds-more'>Getting Time Right: Leap Seconds &#038; More</h2>\n<p>There are supposed to be exactly 24 hours in a day. Except that the Earth doesnt know that. And in fact its rotation period varies slightly with time (generally its rotation is slowing down). So to keep the time of day aligned with where the Sun is in the sky the hack was invented of adding or subtracting <a href='https://www.wolframalpha.com/input/?i=leap+second'>leap seconds</a>.</p>\n<p>In a sense, the problem of describing a moment in time is a bit like the problem of geo location. In geo location theres the question of describing a position in space. Knowing latitude-longitude on the Earth isnt enough; one has to also have a geo model (defined by the <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/GeoModel.html'>GeoModel</a></tt> option) that describes what shape to assume for the Earth, and thus how lat-long should map to actual spatial position.</p>\n<p>In describing a moment of time we similarly have to say how our clock time maps onto actual physical time. And to do that weve introduced in Version 13.0 the notion of a time system, defined by the <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/TimeSystem.html'>TimeSystem</a></tt> option.</p>\n<p>This defines the first moment of December 2021 in the UT1 time system:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='518' height='57' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='13-1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='13-1_out_text'>DateObject[{2021,12,1,0,0,0},TimeSystem->\"UT1\",TimeZone->0]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Heres the first moment of December 2021 in the TAI time system:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img2.png\" alt='' title='' width='513' height='57' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='13-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='13-2_out_text'>DateObject[{2021,12,1,0,0,0},TimeSystem->\"TAI\",TimeZone->0]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>But even though these are both associated with the same clock description, they correspond to different actual moments in time. And subtracting them we get a nonzero value: </p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img3.png\" alt='' title='' width='403' height='57' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img3_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='13-3_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='13-3_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Whats going on here? Well, TAI is a time system based on atomic clocks in which each day is taken to be precisely 24 hours long, and the zero of the time system was set in the late 1950s. UT1, on the other, is a time system in which each day has a length defined by the actual rotation of the Earth. And what this is showing is that in the time since TAI and UT1 were synchronized in the late 1950s the Earths actual rotation has slowed down to the point where it is now about 37 seconds behind where it would be with a precise 24-hour day.</p>\n<p>An important time system is UTCwhich is standard civil time, and the de facto standard time of the internet. UTC doesnt track the precise rotation speed of the Earth; instead it adds or subtracts discrete leap seconds when UT1 is about to accumulate another second of discrepancy from TAIso that right now UTC is exactly 37 seconds behind TAI:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img4.png\" alt='' title='' width='404' height='57' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img4_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='13-4_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='13-4_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>In <a href='https://writings.stephenwolfram.com/2021/05/launching-version-12-3-of-wolfram-language-mathematica/'>Version 12.3</a> we introduced <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/GeoOrientationData.html'>GeoOrientationData</a></tt> which is based on a feed of data on the measured rotation speed of the Earth. Based on this, heres the deviation from 24 hours in the length of day for the past decade:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img5.png\" alt='' title='' width='616' height='275' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img5_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='13-5_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='13-5_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>(And, yes, this shows thatfor the first time since measurements were started in the late 1950sthe Earths rotation is slightly speeding up.)</p>\n<p>Can we see the leap seconds that have been added to account for these changes? Lets look at a few seconds right at the beginning of 2017 in the TAI time system: </p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img6.png\" alt='' title='' width='499' height='100' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img6_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='13-6_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='13-6_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Now lets convert these moments in time into their UTC representationusing the new <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/TimeSystemConvert.html'>TimeSystemConvert</a></tt> function:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img7.png\" alt='' title='' width='422' height='91' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='13-7_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='13-7_out_text'>TimeSystemConvert[#,\"UTC\"]&/@%</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Look carefully at this. First, when 2016 ends and 2017 begins is slightly different in UTC than in TAI. But theres something even weirder going on. At the very end of 2016, UTC shows a time 23:59:60. Why didnt that wrap around in clock arithmetic style to the next day? Answer: because theres a leap second being inserted. (Which makes me wonder just when the New Year was celebrated in time zone 0 that year&#8230;.)</p>\n<p>If you think this is subtle, consider another point. Inside your computer there are lots of timers that control system operationsand that are based on global time. And bad things could happen with these timers if global time glitched. So how can we address this? What we do in Wolfram Language is to use smeared UTC, and effectively smear out the leap second over the course of a dayessentially by making each individual second not exactly a physical second long.</p>\n<p>Heres the beginning of the last second of 2016 in UTC:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img8.png\" alt='' title='' width='501' height='58' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img8_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='13-8_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='13-8_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>But here it is in smeared UTC:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img9.png\" alt='' title='' width='571' height='60' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img9_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='9_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='9_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>And, yes, you can derive that number from the number of seconds in a leap-second day:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/13sw1213img10.png\" alt='' title='' width='167' height='51' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='13-10_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='13-10_out_text'>1/(24 60 60+1.)</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>By the way, you might be wondering why one should care about all this complexity. In everyday life leap seconds are a detail. But if youre doing astronomy they can really matter. After all, in one (leap) second, light goes about 186,000 miles&#8230;.</p>\n<h2 id='new-crisper-geographic-maps'>New, Crisper Geographic Maps</h2>\n<p>Maps involve a lot of data, and efficiently delivering them and rendering them (in appropriate projections, etc.) is a difficult matter. In Version 13.0 were greatly crispening maps, by using vector fonts for all labeling:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/14sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='481' height='286' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/14sw1213img1_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='14-1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='14-1_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>At least for right now, by default the background is still a bitmap. You can use crispened vector graphics for the background as wellbut it will take longer to render:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/14sw1213img2.png\" alt='' title='' width='520' height='286' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/14sw1213img2_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='14-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='14-2_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>One advantage of using vector labels is that they can work in all geo projections (note that in Version 13 if you dont specify the region for <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/GeoGraphics.html'>GeoGraphics</a></tt>, itll default to the whole world):</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/14sw1213img3.png\" alt='' title='' width='481' height='433' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='14-3_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='14-3_out_text'>GeoGraphics[GeoProjection->\"Bonne\"]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Another addition in Version 13 is the ability to mix multiple background layers. Heres an example that includes a street map with a translucent relief map on top (and labels on top of that):</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/14sw1213img4.png\" alt='' title='' width='520' height='469' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/14sw1213img4_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='14-4_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='14-4_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<h2 id='geometric-regions-fitting-and-building'>Geometric Regions: Fitting and Building</h2>\n<p>Given a bunch of points on a circle, what is the circle they are on?</p>\n<p>Here are random points selected around a circle:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img1.png\" alt='' title='' width='612' height='110' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='15-1_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='15-1_out_text'>pts=RandomPoint[Circle[{0,0},2],10]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>The new function <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/RegionFit.html'>RegionFit</a></tt> can figure out what circle the points are on:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img2.png\" alt='' title='' width='201' height='51' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='15-2_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='15-2_out_text'>RegionFit[pts,\"Circle\"]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Heres a collection of points in 3D:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img3.png\" alt='' title='' width='237' height='222' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img3_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='15-3_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='15-3_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>This fits a cylinder to these points:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img4.png\" alt='' title='' width='474' height='228' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='15-4_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='15-4_out_text'>Graphics3D[{pts,Style[RegionFit[pts,\"Cylinder\"],Opacity[.5]]}]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Another very useful new function in Version 13.0 is <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/ConcaveHullMesh.html'>ConcaveHullMesh</a></tt>which attempts to reconstruct a surface from a collection of 3D points. Here are 1000 points:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img5.png\" alt='' title='' width='287' height='300' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL' data-src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img5_copy.txt\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='15-5_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='15-5_out_text'></pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>The convex hull will put shrinkwrap around everything:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img6.png\" alt='' title='' width='209' height='235' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='15-6_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='15-6_out_text'>ConvexHullMesh[pts]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>But the concave hull will make the surface go into the concavities:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img7.png\" alt='' title='' width='260' height='299' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='15-7_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='15-7_out_text'>ConcaveHullMesh[pts]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Theres a lot of freedom in how one can reconstruct the surface. Another function in Version 13 is <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/GradientFittedMesh.html'>GradientFittedMesh</a></tt>, which forms the surface from a collection of inferred surface normals:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img8.png\" alt='' title='' width='251' height='273' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='15-8_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='15-8_out_text'>GradientFittedMesh[pts]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Weve just talked about finding geometric regions from point data. Another new capability in Version 13.0 is <a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/guide/SolidGeometry.html#1393753078'>constructive solid geometry (CSG)</a>, which explicitly builds up regions from geometric primitives. The main function is <tt><a href='https://reference.wolfram.com/language/ref/CSGRegion.html'>CSGRegion</a></tt>, which allows a variety of operations to be done on primitives. Heres a region formed from an intersection of primitives:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img9.png\" alt='' title='' width='345' height='250' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='15-9_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='15-9_out_text'>CSGRegion[\"Intersection\",{Ball[],Cuboid[]}]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>Note that this is an exact regionno numerical approximation is involved. So when we ask for its volume, we get an exact result:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' id='15-10_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text' id='15-10_out_text'>Volume[%]</pre>\n</p></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>One can build up more complicated structures hierarchically:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img11.png\" alt='' title='' width='575' height='290' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' integrals get difficult, its still often possible to get exact results for things like volume:</p>\n<table class='InCell'>\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td colspan='1' rowspan='1' class='Input'>\n<div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<img loading='lazy' class='' src=\"https://content.wolfram.com/uploads/sites/43/2021/12/15sw1213img12.png\" alt='' title='' width='126' height='71' />\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n<div class='IFL'\n\t\t\t\t\t<span class='close' id='15-12_out'>&#38;#10005</span></p>\n<pre class='text'
